<li><a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Bible" title="Criticism of the Bible">Criticism of the Bible</a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">
<p><a href="/wiki/Outline_of_Bible-related_topics" title="Outline of Bible-related topics">Outline of Bible-related topics</a></p>
<img alt="Wikipedia book" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Symbol_book_class2.svg/16px-Symbol_book_class2.svg.png" title="Wikipedia book" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Symbol_book_class2.svg/23px-Symbol_book_class2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Symbol_book_class2.svg/31px-Symbol_book_class2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="185" />&#160;<a href="/wiki/Book:Bible" title="Book:Bible">Bible book</a>&#160; &#160; <a href="/wiki/File:Bible.malmesbury.arp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Bible.malmesbury.arp.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bible.malmesbury.arp.jpg/32px-Bible.malmesbury.arp.jpg" width="32" height="21" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bible.malmesbury.arp.jpg/48px-Bible.malmesbury.arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bible.malmesbury.arp.jpg/64px-Bible.malmesbury.arp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1993" data-file-height="1300" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Bible" title="Portal:Bible">Bible portal</a></td>

<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Bible_sidebar" title="Template:Bible sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>

<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Bible_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Bible</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a> τὰ βιβλία, <i>tà <a href="/wiki/Bibl%C3%ADa" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblía">biblía</a></i>, "the books")<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> is a collection of <a href="/wiki/Sacred_texts" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacred texts">sacred texts</a> or scriptures that <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christians</a> consider to be a <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">product of divine inspiration</a> and a record of the relationship between <a href="/wiki/God_in_Abrahamic_religions" title="God in Abrahamic religions">God</a> and humans.</p>
<p>Many different authors contributed to the Bible. What is regarded as <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">canonical</a> text differs depending on traditions and groups; a number of Bible canons have evolved, with overlapping and diverging contents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiches20007.E2.80.938_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiches20007.E2.80.938-2">[2]</a></sup> The Christian <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> overlaps with the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> and the Greek <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a>; the Hebrew Bible is known in <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>. The <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> is a collection of writings by <a href="/wiki/Early_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Christians">early Christians</a>, believed to be mostly <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Christian" title="Jewish Christian">Jewish disciples of Christ</a>, written in first-century <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>. These early Christian Greek writings consist of narratives, <a href="/wiki/Epistle" title="Epistle">letters</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptic_literature" title="Apocalyptic literature">apocalyptic writings</a>. Among <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denominations</a> there is some disagreement about the contents of the canon, primarily the <a href="/wiki/Apocrypha" title="Apocrypha">Apocrypha</a>, a list of works that are regarded with varying levels of respect.</p>
<p>Attitudes towards the Bible also differ amongst Christian groups. <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholics" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholics">Roman Catholics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> Christians stress the harmony and importance of the Bible and <a href="/wiki/Sacred_tradition" title="Sacred tradition">sacred tradition</a>, while <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> churches focus on the idea of <i><a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">sola scriptura</a></i>, or scripture alone. This concept arose during the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, and many denominations today support the use of the Bible as the only source of Christian teaching.</p>
<p>With estimated total sales of over 5 billion copies, the Bible is widely considered to be the best-selling book of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> It has estimated annual sales of 100 million copies,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RussellAsh_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RussellAsh-6">[6]</a></sup> and has been a major influence on literature and history, especially in <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">the West</a>, where the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">Gutenberg Bible</a> was the first book printed using <a href="/wiki/Movable_type" title="Movable type">movable type</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The English word <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Bible" class="extiw" title="wikt:Bible">Bible</a></i> is from the Latin <i>biblia</i>, from the same word in <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a> and ultimately from <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a> <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">τὰ βιβλία</span> <i>ta biblia</i> "the books" (singular <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">βιβλίον</span> <i>biblion</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-etymonline-bible_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline-bible-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> <i>biblia</i> is short for <i>biblia sacra</i> "holy book", while <i>biblia</i> in Greek and Late Latin is neuter plural (gen. <i>bibliorum</i>). It gradually came to be regarded as a feminine singular noun (<i>biblia</i>, gen. <i>bibliae</i>) in medieval Latin, and so the word was loaned as a singular into the vernaculars of Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Latin <i>biblia sacra</i> "holy books" translates Greek <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">τὰ βιβλία τὰ ἅγια</span> <i>ta biblia ta hagia</i>, "the holy books".<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>The word <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">βιβλίον</span> itself had the literal meaning of "paper" or "scroll" and came to be used as the ordinary word for "book". It is the diminutive of <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">βύβλος</span> <i>byblos</i>, "Egyptian papyrus", possibly so called from the name of the <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> sea port <a href="/wiki/Byblos" title="Byblos">Byblos</a> (also known as Gebal) from whence Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">papyrus</a> was exported to Greece. The Greek <i>ta biblia</i> (lit. "little papyrus books")<sup id="cite_ref-Stagg_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stagg-10">[10]</a></sup> was "an expression <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Hellenistic Jews">Hellenistic Jews</a> used to describe their sacred books (the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Christian use of the term can be traced to c. 223 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-etymonline-bible_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-etymonline-bible-7">[7]</a></sup> The biblical scholar <a href="/wiki/F.F._Bruce" class="mw-redirect" title="F.F. Bruce">F.F. Bruce</a> notes that <a href="/wiki/Chrysostom" class="mw-redirect" title="Chrysostom">Chrysostom</a> appears to be the first writer (in his <i>Homilies on Matthew</i>, delivered between 386 and 388) to use the Greek phrase <i>ta biblia</i> ("the books") to describe both the Old and New Testaments together.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1988214_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1988214-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textual_history">Textual history</span></h3>
<p>By the 2nd century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">BCE</a>, Jewish groups began calling the books of the Bible the "scriptures" and they referred to them as "holy", or in Hebrew כִּתְבֵי הַקֹּדֶשׁ (Kitvei hakkodesh), and Christians now commonly call the Old and New Testaments of the Christian Bible "The Holy Bible" (in Greek <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">τὰ βιβλία τὰ ἅγια</span>, <i>tà biblía tà ágia</i>) or "the Holy Scriptures" (<span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">η Αγία Γραφή</span>, <i>e Agía Graphḗ</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The Bible was <a href="/wiki/Chapters_and_verses_of_the_Bible" title="Chapters and verses of the Bible">divided into chapters</a> in the 13th century by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Langton" title="Stephen Langton">Stephen Langton</a> and it was divided into verses in the 16th century by French printer <a href="/wiki/Robert_Estienne" title="Robert Estienne">Robert Estienne</a><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and is now usually <a href="/wiki/Citation" title="Citation">cited</a> by book, chapter, and verse. The division of the Hebrew Bible into verses is based on the <i><a href="/wiki/Sof_passuk" title="Sof passuk">sof passuk</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Cantillation" title="Cantillation">cantillation</a> mark used by the 10th-century <a href="/wiki/Masoretes" title="Masoretes">Masoretes</a> to record the verse divisions used in earlier oral traditions.</p>
<p>The oldest extant copy of a complete Bible is an early 4th-century parchment book preserved in the <a href="/wiki/Vatican_Library" title="Vatican Library">Vatican Library</a>, and it is known as the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus" title="Codex Vaticanus">Codex Vaticanus</a>. The oldest copy of the <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> in Hebrew and Aramaic dates from the 10th century CE. The oldest copy of a complete Latin (<a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>) Bible is the <a href="/wiki/Codex_Amiatinus" title="Codex Amiatinus">Codex Amiatinus</a>, dating from the 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Authorship_of_the_Bible" title="Authorship of the Bible">Authorship of the Bible</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Isaiah_Scroll.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Great_Isaiah_Scroll.jpg/300px-Great_Isaiah_Scroll.jpg" width="300" height="95" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Great_Isaiah_Scroll.jpg/450px-Great_Isaiah_Scroll.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Great_Isaiah_Scroll.jpg/600px-Great_Isaiah_Scroll.jpg 2x" data-file-width="12540" data-file-height="3960" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Isaiah_Scroll.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_scroll" title="Isaiah scroll">Isaiah scroll</a>, which is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea_Scrolls" title="Dead Sea Scrolls">Dead Sea Scrolls</a>, contains almost the whole <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Isaiah" title="Book of Isaiah">Book of Isaiah</a>. It dates from the 2nd century BCE.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/220px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/330px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg/440px-File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="445" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:File%22-Saint_Paul_Writing_His_Epistles%22_by_Valentin_de_Boulogne.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Saint Paul Writing His Epistles</i>, 16th-century painting.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Professor John K. Riches, <a href="/wiki/Professor_of_Divinity_and_Biblical_Criticism" title="Professor of Divinity and Biblical Criticism">Professor of Divinity and Biblical Criticism</a> at the University of Glasgow, says that "the biblical texts themselves are the result of a creative dialogue between ancient traditions and different communities through the ages",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiches200083_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiches200083-17">[17]</a></sup> and "the biblical texts were produced over a period in which the living conditions of the writers – political, cultural, economic, and ecological – varied enormously".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiches20009_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiches20009-18">[18]</a></sup> Timothy H. Lim, a professor of Hebrew Bible and Second Temple Judaism at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Edinburgh" title="University of Edinburgh">University of Edinburgh</a>, says that the <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a> is "a collection of authoritative texts of apparently divine origin that went through a human process of writing and editing."<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> He states that it is not a magical book, nor was it literally written by <a href="/wiki/God_in_Judaism" title="God in Judaism">God</a> and passed to mankind. Parallel to the solidification of the Hebrew canon (c. 3rd century BCE), only the Torah first and then the Tanakh began to be translated into Greek and expanded, now referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> or the Greek Old Testament.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiches200037_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiches200037-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Christian Bibles, the New Testament Gospels were derived from oral traditions in the second half of the first century CE. Riches says that:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Scholars have attempted to reconstruct something of the history of the oral traditions behind the Gospels, but the results have not been too encouraging. The period of transmission is short: less than 40 years passed between the death of Jesus and the writing of Mark's Gospel. This means that there was little time for oral traditions to assume fixed form.<sup id="cite_ref-Riches_2000_23.2C_37_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riches_2000_23.2C_37-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The Bible was later translated into Latin and other languages. John Riches states that:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>The translation of the Bible into Latin marks the beginning of a parting of the ways between Western Latin-speaking Christianity and Eastern Christianity, which spoke Greek, Syriac, Coptic, Ethiopic, and other languages. The Bibles of the Eastern Churches vary considerably: the Ethiopic Orthodox canon includes 81 books and contains many apocalyptic texts, such as were found at Qumran and subsequently excluded from the Jewish canon. As a general rule, one can say that the Orthodox Churches generally follow the Septuagint in including more books in their Old Testaments than are in the Jewish canon.<sup id="cite_ref-Riches_2000_23.2C_37_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riches_2000_23.2C_37-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hebrew_Bible">Hebrew Bible</span></h2>
<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks plainlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%;line-height:1.4em;">

<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> and <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_Hebrew_Bible_canon" title="Development of the Hebrew Bible canon">Development of the Hebrew Bible canon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2nd_century_Hebrew_decalogue.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/2nd_century_Hebrew_decalogue.jpg/170px-2nd_century_Hebrew_decalogue.jpg" width="170" height="275" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/2nd_century_Hebrew_decalogue.jpg/255px-2nd_century_Hebrew_decalogue.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/2nd_century_Hebrew_decalogue.jpg/340px-2nd_century_Hebrew_decalogue.jpg 2x" data-file-width="832" data-file-height="1348" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2nd_century_Hebrew_decalogue.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Nash_Papyrus" title="Nash Papyrus">Nash Papyrus</a> (2nd century BCE) contains a portion of a pre-Masoretic Text, specifically the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Shema_Yisrael" title="Shema Yisrael">Shema Yisrael</a> prayer.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Masoretic_Text" title="Masoretic Text">Masoretic Text</a> is the authoritative <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Hebrew</a> text of the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a>. It defines the books of the Jewish canon, and also the precise letter-text of these biblical books, with their <a href="/wiki/Niqqud" title="Niqqud">vocalization</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cantillation" title="Cantillation">accentuation</a>.</p>
<p>The oldest extant manuscripts of the Masoretic Text date from approximately the 9th century CE,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Aleppo_Codex" title="Aleppo Codex">Aleppo Codex</a> (once the oldest complete copy of the Masoretic Text, but now missing its <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> section) dates from the 10th century.</p>
<p>The name <a href="/wiki/Tanakh" title="Tanakh">Tanakh</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span class="script-hebrew" style="font-size: 115%; font-family: Alef, 'SBL BibLit', 'SBL Hebrew', 'David CLM', 'Frenk Ruehl CLM', 'Hadasim CLM', Shofar, 'Ezra SIL', 'Ezra SIL SR', Cardo, 'Noto Sans Hebrew', David, FreeSerif, 'Times New Roman', FreeSans, Arial;" dir="rtl">תנ"ך</span>‎) reflects the threefold division of the Hebrew Scriptures, <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a> ("Teaching"), <a href="/wiki/Nevi%27im" title="Nevi'im">Nevi'im</a> ("Prophets") and <a href="/wiki/Ketuvim" title="Ketuvim">Ketuvim</a> ("Writings").</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Torah">Torah</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Oral_Torah" title="Oral Torah">Oral Torah</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:K%C3%B6ln-Tora-und-Innenansicht-Synagoge-Glockengasse-040.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/K%C3%B6ln-Tora-und-Innenansicht-Synagoge-Glockengasse-040.JPG/220px-K%C3%B6ln-Tora-und-Innenansicht-Synagoge-Glockengasse-040.JPG" width="220" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/K%C3%B6ln-Tora-und-Innenansicht-Synagoge-Glockengasse-040.JPG/330px-K%C3%B6ln-Tora-und-Innenansicht-Synagoge-Glockengasse-040.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/K%C3%B6ln-Tora-und-Innenansicht-Synagoge-Glockengasse-040.JPG/440px-K%C3%B6ln-Tora-und-Innenansicht-Synagoge-Glockengasse-040.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1496" data-file-height="2040" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:K%C3%B6ln-Tora-und-Innenansicht-Synagoge-Glockengasse-040.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Torah_scroll" class="mw-redirect" title="Torah scroll">Torah scroll</a> recovered from <a href="/wiki/Glockengasse_Synagogue" title="Glockengasse Synagogue">Glockengasse Synagogue</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Torah (תּוֹרָה) is also known as the "Five Books of <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>" or the <a href="/wiki/Pentateuch" class="mw-redirect" title="Pentateuch">Pentateuch</a>, meaning "five scroll-cases".<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Samaritan_Inscription_containing_portion_of_the_Bible_in_nine_lines_of_Hebrew_text,_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Samaritan_Inscription_containing_portion_of_the_Bible_in_nine_lines_of_Hebrew_text%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg/220px-Samaritan_Inscription_containing_portion_of_the_Bible_in_nine_lines_of_Hebrew_text%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg" width="220" height="242" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Samaritan_Inscription_containing_portion_of_the_Bible_in_nine_lines_of_Hebrew_text%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg/330px-Samaritan_Inscription_containing_portion_of_the_Bible_in_nine_lines_of_Hebrew_text%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Samaritan_Inscription_containing_portion_of_the_Bible_in_nine_lines_of_Hebrew_text%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg/440px-Samaritan_Inscription_containing_portion_of_the_Bible_in_nine_lines_of_Hebrew_text%2C_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4016" data-file-height="4420" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Samaritan_Inscription_containing_portion_of_the_Bible_in_nine_lines_of_Hebrew_text,_currently_housed_in_the_British_Museum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Samaritan Inscription containing portion of the Bible in nine lines of Hebrew text, currently housed in the British Museum</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Hebrew names of the books are derived from the <a href="/wiki/Incipit" title="Incipit">first words</a> in the respective texts. The Torah consists of the following five books:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Genesis" title="Book of Genesis">Genesis</a>, <i>Beresheeth</i> (בראשית)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Exodus</a>, <i>Shemot</i> (שמות)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Leviticus" title="Book of Leviticus">Leviticus</a>, <i>Vayikra</i> (ויקרא)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Numbers" title="Book of Numbers">Numbers</a>, <i>Bamidbar</i> (במדבר)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Deuteronomy" title="Book of Deuteronomy">Deuteronomy</a>, <i>Devarim</i> (דברים)</li>
</ul>
<p>The first eleven chapters of Genesis provide accounts of the <a href="/wiki/Genesis_creation_narrative" title="Genesis creation narrative">creation</a> (or ordering) of the world and the history of God's early relationship with humanity. The remaining thirty-nine chapters of Genesis provide an account of God's <a href="/wiki/Biblical_covenant" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical covenant">covenant</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Patriarchs_(Bible)" title="Patriarchs (Bible)">Biblical patriarchs</a> <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac" title="Isaac">Isaac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jacob" title="Jacob">Jacob</a> (also called <a href="/wiki/Israel_(name)" title="Israel (name)">Israel</a>) and Jacob's children, the "<a href="/wiki/Children_of_Israel" class="mw-redirect" title="Children of Israel">Children of Israel</a>", especially <a href="/wiki/Joseph_(Hebrew_Bible)" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph (Hebrew Bible)">Joseph</a>. It tells of how God commanded Abraham to leave his family and home in the city of <a href="/wiki/Ur_of_the_Chaldees" title="Ur of the Chaldees">Ur</a>, eventually to settle in the land of <a href="/wiki/Canaan" title="Canaan">Canaan</a>, and how the Children of Israel later moved to Egypt. The remaining four books of the Torah tell the story of <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a>, who lived hundreds of years after the patriarchs. He leads the Children of Israel from slavery in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a> to the renewal of their covenant with God at <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Mount_Sinai" title="Biblical Mount Sinai">Mount Sinai</a> and their wanderings in the desert until a new generation was ready to enter the land of Canaan. The Torah ends with the death of Moses.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Torah contains the commandments of God, revealed at Mount Sinai (although there is some debate among traditional scholars as to whether these were all written down at one time, or over a period of time during the 40 years of the wanderings in the desert, while several modern Jewish movements reject the idea of a literal revelation, and critical scholars believe that many of these laws developed later in Jewish history).<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> These commandments provide the basis for <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">Jewish religious law</a>. Tradition states that there are <a href="/wiki/613_commandments" title="613 commandments">613 commandments</a> (<i>taryag mitzvot</i>).</p>
<h3><span id="Nevi'im"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nevi.27im">Nevi'im</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Nevi%27im" title="Nevi'im">Nevi'im</a></div>

<tr>
<td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top:1px solid #aaa;padding-top:0.15em;border-bottom:1px solid #aaa;"><a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a></td>

</div>

<p><i>Nevi'im</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>: <span lang="he" dir="rtl" xml:lang="he">נְבִיאִים <i>Nəḇî'îm</i></span>‎‎, "Prophets") is the second main division of the Tanakh, between the <i>Torah</i> and <i>Ketuvim.</i> It contains two sub-groups, the Former Prophets (<span lang="he" xml:lang="he"><i>Nevi'im Rishonim</i> נביאים ראשונים</span>, the narrative books of Joshua, Judges, Samuel and Kings) and the Latter Prophets (<span lang="he" xml:lang="he"><i>Nevi'im Aharonim</i> נביאים אחרונים</span>, the books of Isaiah, Jeremiah and Ezekiel and the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Minor_Prophets" title="Twelve Minor Prophets">Twelve Minor Prophets</a>).</p>
<p>The Nevi'im tell the story of the rise of the Hebrew monarchy and its division into two kingdoms, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Israel_and_Judah" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Israel and Judah">ancient Israel and Judah</a>, focusing on conflicts between the Israelites and other nations, and conflicts among Israelites, specifically, struggles between believers in "the L<span class="smallcaps" style="font-size:83%">ORD</span> God"<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and believers in foreign gods,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and the criticism of unethical and unjust behaviour of Israelite elites and rulers;<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> in which prophets played a crucial and leading role. It ends with the conquest of the Kingdom of Israel by the Assyrians followed by the conquest of the Kingdom of Judah by the Babylonians and the destruction of the Temple in Jerusalem.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_Prophets">Former Prophets</span></h4>
<p>The Former Prophets are the books Joshua, Judges, Samuel and Kings. They contain narratives that begin immediately after the death of Moses with the divine appointment of Joshua as his successor, who then leads the people of Israel into the <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">Promised Land</a>, and end with the release from imprisonment of the last <a href="/wiki/King_of_Judah" class="mw-redirect" title="King of Judah">king of Judah</a>. Treating Samuel and Kings as single books, they cover:</p>
<ul>
<li>Joshua's conquest of the land of Canaan (in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Joshua" title="Book of Joshua">Book of Joshua</a>),</li>
<li>the struggle of the people to possess the land (in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Judges" title="Book of Judges">Book of Judges</a>),</li>
<li>the people's request to God to give them a king so that they can occupy the land in the face of their enemies (in the <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Samuel" title="Books of Samuel">Books of Samuel</a>)</li>
<li>the possession of the land under the divinely appointed kings of the House of David, ending in conquest and foreign exile (<a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">Books of Kings</a>)</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Latter_Prophets">Latter Prophets</span></h4>
<p>The Latter Prophets are divided into two groups, the "major" prophets, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Isaiah" title="Book of Isaiah">Isaiah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jeremiah" title="Book of Jeremiah">Jeremiah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ezekiel" title="Book of Ezekiel">Ezekiel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Daniel</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Minor_Prophets" title="Twelve Minor Prophets">Twelve Minor Prophets</a>, collected into a single book. The collection is broken up to form twelve individual books in the Christian <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>, one for each of the prophets:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Hosea" title="Book of Hosea">Hosea</a>, <i>Hoshea</i> (הושע)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Joel" title="Book of Joel">Joel</a>, <i>Yoel</i> (יואל)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Amos" title="Book of Amos">Amos</a>, <i>Amos</i> (עמוס)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Obadiah" title="Book of Obadiah">Obadiah</a>, <i>Ovadyah</i> (עבדיה)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jonah" title="Book of Jonah">Jonah</a>, <i>Yonah</i> (יונה)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Micah" title="Book of Micah">Micah</a>, <i>Mikhah</i> (מיכה)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Nahum" title="Book of Nahum">Nahum</a>, <i>Nahum</i> (נחום)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Habakkuk" title="Book of Habakkuk">Habakkuk</a>, <i>Havakuk</i> (חבקוק)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Zephaniah" title="Book of Zephaniah">Zephaniah</a>, <i>Tsefanya</i> (צפניה)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Haggai" title="Book of Haggai">Haggai</a>, <i>Khagay</i> (חגי)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Zechariah" title="Book of Zechariah">Zechariah</a>, <i>Zekharyah</i> (זכריה)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Malachi" title="Book of Malachi">Malachi</a>, <i>Malakhi</i> (מלאכי)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ketuvim">Ketuvim</span></h3>

<p><i>Ketuvim</i> or <i>Kəṯûḇîm</i> (in <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical Hebrew language">Biblical Hebrew</a>: <span lang="hbo" dir="rtl" xml:lang="hbo">כְּתוּבִים</span>‎‎ "writings") is the third and final section of the Tanakh. The Ketuvim are believed to have been written under the <a href="/wiki/Ruach_HaKodesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Ruach HaKodesh">Ruach HaKodesh</a> (the Holy Spirit) but with one level less authority than that of <a href="/wiki/Prophecy" title="Prophecy">prophecy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_poetic_books">The poetic books</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bhs_psalm1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Bhs_psalm1.png/220px-Bhs_psalm1.png" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/Bhs_psalm1.png 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bhs_psalm1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Hebrew" class="mw-redirect" title="Hebrew">Hebrew</a> text of <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://bible.oremus.org/?passage=Psalms+1:1–2:1&amp;version=nrsv">Psalm 1:1–2</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Masoretic" class="mw-redirect" title="Masoretic">Masoretic</a> manuscripts (and some printed editions), Psalms, Proverbs and Job are presented in a special two-column form emphasizing the parallel stichs in the verses, which are a function of their <a href="/wiki/Biblical_poetry" title="Biblical poetry">poetry</a>. Collectively, these three books are known as <i>Sifrei Emet</i> (an acronym of the titles in Hebrew, איוב, משלי, תהלים yields <i>Emet</i> אמ"ת, which is also the Hebrew for "truth").</p>
<p>These three books are also the only ones in Tanakh with a special system of <a href="/wiki/Cantillation" title="Cantillation">cantillation</a> notes that are designed to emphasize parallel stichs within verses. However, the beginning and end of the book of Job are in the normal prose system.</p>
<h4><span id="The_five_scrolls_(Hamesh_Megillot)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_five_scrolls_.28Hamesh_Megillot.29">The five scrolls (<i>Hamesh Megillot</i>)</span></h4>
<p>The five relatively short books of <a href="/wiki/Song_of_Songs" title="Song of Songs">Song of Songs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ruth" title="Book of Ruth">Book of Ruth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Lamentations" title="Book of Lamentations">Book of Lamentations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastes" title="Ecclesiastes">Ecclesiastes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Esther" title="Book of Esther">Book of Esther</a> are collectively known as the <i>Hamesh Megillot</i> (<a href="/wiki/Five_Megillot" title="Five Megillot">Five Megillot</a>). These are the latest books collected and designated as "authoritative" in the Jewish canon even though they were not complete until the 2nd century CE.<sup id="cite_ref-Coogan2009_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coogan2009-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_books">Other books</span></h4>
<p>Besides the three poetic books and the five scrolls, the remaining books in Ketuvim are <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Daniel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ezra%E2%80%93Nehemiah" title="Ezra–Nehemiah">Ezra–Nehemiah</a> and Chronicles. Although there is no formal grouping for these books in the Jewish tradition, they nevertheless share a number of distinguishing characteristics:</p>
<ul>
<li>Their narratives all openly describe relatively late events (i.e., the Babylonian captivity and the subsequent restoration of Zion).</li>
<li>The Talmudic tradition ascribes late authorship to all of them.</li>
<li>Two of them (Daniel and Ezra) are the only books in the Tanakh with significant portions in <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Aramaic" title="Biblical Aramaic">Aramaic</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Order_of_the_books">Order of the books</span></h4>
<p>The following list presents the books of Ketuvim in the order they appear in most printed editions. It also divides them into three subgroups based on the distinctiveness of <i>Sifrei Emet</i> and <i>Hamesh Megillot</i>.</p>
<p>The Three Poetic Books (<i>Sifrei Emet</i>)</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Tehillim</i> (<a href="/wiki/Psalms" title="Psalms">Psalms</a>) תְהִלִּים</li>
<li><i>Mishlei</i> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Proverbs" title="Book of Proverbs">Book of Proverbs</a>) מִשְלֵי</li>
<li><i>Iyyôbh</i> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Job" title="Book of Job">Book of Job</a>) אִיּוֹב</li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Five_Megillot" title="Five Megillot">Five Megillot</a> (<i>Hamesh Megillot</i>)</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Shīr Hashshīrīm</i> (<a href="/wiki/Song_of_Songs" title="Song of Songs">Song of Songs</a>) or (Song of Solomon) שִׁיר הַשׁשִׁירִים (<a href="/wiki/Passover" title="Passover">Passover</a>)</li>
<li><i>Rūth</i> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ruth" title="Book of Ruth">Book of Ruth</a>) רוּת (<a href="/wiki/Sh%C4%81bh%C3%BB%E2%80%98%C3%B4th" class="mw-redirect" title="Shābhû‘ôth">Shābhû‘ôth</a>)</li>
<li><i>Eikhah</i> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Lamentations" title="Book of Lamentations">Lamentations</a>) איכה (<a href="/wiki/Ninth_of_Av" class="mw-redirect" title="Ninth of Av">Ninth of Av</a>) [Also called <i>Kinnot</i> in Hebrew.]</li>
<li><i>Qōheleth</i> (<a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastes" title="Ecclesiastes">Ecclesiastes</a>) קהלת (<a href="/wiki/Sukk%C3%B4th" class="mw-redirect" title="Sukkôth">Sukkôth</a>)</li>
<li><i>Estēr</i> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Esther" title="Book of Esther">Book of Esther</a>) אֶסְתֵר (<a href="/wiki/P%C3%BBr%C3%AEm" class="mw-redirect" title="Pûrîm">Pûrîm</a>)</li>
</ul>
<p>Other books</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Dānî’ēl</i> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Book of Daniel</a>) דָּנִיֵּאל</li>
<li><i>‘Ezrā</i> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Ezra" title="Book of Ezra">Book of Ezra</a>–<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Nehemiah" title="Book of Nehemiah">Book of Nehemiah</a>) עזרא</li>
<li><i>Divrei ha-Yamim</i> (<a href="/wiki/Books_of_Chronicles" title="Books of Chronicles">Chronicles</a>) דברי הימים</li>
</ul>
<p>The Jewish textual tradition never finalized the order of the books in Ketuvim. The <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">Babylonian Talmud</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bava_Batra" title="Bava Batra">Bava Batra</a> 14b–15a) gives their order as Ruth, Psalms, Job, Proverbs, Ecclesiastes, Song of Solomon, Lamentations of Jeremiah, Daniel, Scroll of Esther, Ezra, Chronicles.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Tiberian <a href="/wiki/Masoretic" class="mw-redirect" title="Masoretic">Masoretic</a> codices, including the <a href="/wiki/Aleppo_Codex" title="Aleppo Codex">Aleppo Codex</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Leningrad_Codex" title="Leningrad Codex">Leningrad Codex</a>, and often in old Spanish manuscripts as well, the order is Chronicles, Psalms, Job, Proverbs, Ruth, Song of Solomon, Ecclesiastes, Lamentations of Jeremiah, Esther, Daniel, Ezra.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canonization">Canonization</span></h4>
<p>The Ketuvim is the last of the three portions of the Tanakh to have been accepted as <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">biblical canon</a>. While the Torah may have been considered canon by Israel as early as the 5th century BCE and the <a href="/wiki/Former_Prophets" class="mw-redirect" title="Former Prophets">Former</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latter_Prophets" class="mw-redirect" title="Latter Prophets">Latter Prophets</a> were canonized by the 2nd century BCE, the Ketuvim was not a fixed canon until the 2nd century of the <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common Era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Coogan2009_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coogan2009-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Evidence suggests, however, that the people of Israel were adding what would become the Ketuvim to their holy literature shortly after the canonization of the prophets. As early as 132 BCE references suggest that the Ketuvim was starting to take shape, although it lacked a formal title.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenshaw196316.E2.80.9317_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenshaw196316.E2.80.9317-39">[39]</a></sup> References in the four <a href="/wiki/Gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Gospels">Gospels</a> as well as other books of <a href="/wiki/The_New_Testament" class="mw-redirect" title="The New Testament">the New Testament</a> indicate that many of these texts were both commonly known and counted as having some degree of religious authority early in the 1st century CE.</p>
<p>Many scholars believe that the limits of the Ketuvim as canonized scripture were determined by the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Jamnia" title="Council of Jamnia">Council of Jamnia</a> c. 90 CE. <i><a href="/wiki/Against_Apion" title="Against Apion">Against Apion</a></i>, the writing of <a href="/wiki/Flavius_Josephus" class="mw-redirect" title="Flavius Josephus">Josephus</a> in 95 CE, treated the text of the Hebrew Bible as a closed canon to which "... no one has ventured either to add, or to remove, or to alter a syllable..."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> For a long time following this date the divine inspiration of Esther, <a href="/wiki/The_Song_of_Songs" class="mw-redirect" title="The Song of Songs">the Song of Songs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastes" title="Ecclesiastes">Ecclesiastes</a> was often under scrutiny.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenshaw196317_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenshaw196317-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_languages">Original languages</span></h3>
<p>The Tanakh was mainly written in <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">biblical Hebrew</a>, with some small portions (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Ezra+4%3A8%E2%80%936%3A18&amp;version=NRSV">Ezra 4:8–6:18</a></span> and <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Ezra+7%3A12%E2%80%9326&amp;version=NRSV">7:12–26</a></span>, <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jeremiah+10%3A11&amp;version=NRSV">Jeremiah 10:11</a></span>, <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Daniel+2%3A4%E2%80%937%3A28&amp;version=NRSV">Daniel 2:4–7:28</a></span>) written in <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Aramaic" title="Biblical Aramaic">biblical Aramaic</a>, a sister language which became the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> for much of the Semitic world.<sup id="cite_ref-Driver_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Driver-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Septuagint">Septuagint</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Codex_vaticanus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Codex_vaticanus.jpg/170px-Codex_vaticanus.jpg" width="170" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Codex_vaticanus.jpg/255px-Codex_vaticanus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Codex_vaticanus.jpg/340px-Codex_vaticanus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="438" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Codex_vaticanus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Fragment of a Septuagint: A column of <a href="/wiki/Uncial" class="mw-redirect" title="Uncial">uncial</a> book from <a href="/wiki/1_Esdras" title="1 Esdras">1 Esdras</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/Codex_Vaticanus_Graecus_1209" class="mw-redirect" title="Codex Vaticanus Graecus 1209">Codex Vaticanus</a></i> c. 325–350&#160;CE, the basis of Sir Lancelot Charles Lee Brenton's Greek edition and <a href="/wiki/Brenton%27s_English_Translation_of_the_Septuagint" class="mw-redirect" title="Brenton's English Translation of the Septuagint">English translation</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a>, or the LXX, is a translation of the Hebrew Scriptures and some related texts into <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>, begun in the late 3rd century BCE and completed by 132&#160;BCE,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ndq_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ndq-45">[45]</a></sup> initially in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, but in time it was completed elsewhere as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Jobes_and_Silva_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jobes_and_Silva-46">[46]</a></sup> It is not altogether clear which was translated when, or where; some may even have been translated twice, into different versions, and then revised.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the work of translation progressed, the canon of the Greek Bible expanded. The Torah always maintained its pre-eminence as the basis of the canon but the collection of prophetic writings, based on the <i>Nevi'im</i>, had various <a href="/wiki/Hagiographa" class="mw-redirect" title="Hagiographa">hagiographical</a> works incorporated into it. In addition, some newer books were included in the Septuagint, among these are the <a href="/wiki/Books_of_the_Maccabees" title="Books of the Maccabees">Maccabees</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Wisdom_of_Sirach" class="mw-redirect" title="Wisdom of Sirach">Wisdom of Sirach</a>. However, the book of <a href="/wiki/Sirach" title="Sirach">Sirach</a>, is now known to have existed in a Hebrew version, since ancient Hebrew manuscripts of it were rediscovered in modern times. The Septuagint version of some Biblical books, like <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Daniel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Esther" title="Book of Esther">Esther</a>, are longer than those in the Jewish canon.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Some of these <a href="/wiki/Deuterocanonical_books" title="Deuterocanonical books">deuterocanonical books</a> (e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Wisdom" title="Book of Wisdom">Wisdom of Solomon</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/2_Maccabees" title="2 Maccabees">second book of Maccabees</a>) were not translated, but composed directly in Greek.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Since <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a>, once attributed to a hypothetical late 1st-century <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Jamnia#Developments_attributed_to_Jamnia" title="Council of Jamnia">Council of Jamnia</a>, mainstream <a href="/wiki/Rabbinic_Judaism" title="Rabbinic Judaism">Rabbinic Judaism</a> rejected the Septuagint as valid Jewish scriptural texts. Several reasons have been given for this. First, some mistranslations were claimed. Second, the Hebrew source texts used for the Septuagint differed from the Masoretic tradition of Hebrew texts, which was chosen as canonical by the Jewish rabbis.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Third, the rabbis wanted to distinguish their tradition from the newly emerging tradition of Christianity.<sup id="cite_ref-ndq_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ndq-45">[45]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Finally, the rabbis claimed a divine authority for the Hebrew language, in contrast to Aramaic or Greek – even though these languages were the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of Jews during this period (and Aramaic would eventually be given a holy language status comparable to Hebrew).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Septuagint is the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Vetus_Latina" title="Vetus Latina">Old Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Church Slavonic language">Slavonic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syro-hexaplar_version" title="Syro-hexaplar version">Syriac</a>, Old <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, Old <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a> versions of the Christian <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-W.C3.BCrthwein_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-W.C3.BCrthwein-52">[52]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> Churches use most of the books of the Septuagint, while <a href="/wiki/Protestant" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant">Protestant</a> churches usually do not. After the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, many Protestant Bibles began to follow the Jewish canon and exclude the additional texts, which came to be called <a href="/wiki/Biblical_apocrypha" title="Biblical apocrypha">Biblical apocrypha</a>. The Apocrypha are included under a separate heading in the <a href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorized King James Version">King James Version</a> of the Bible, the basis for the <a href="/wiki/Revised_Standard_Version" title="Revised Standard Version">Revised Standard Version</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Incorporations_from_Theodotion">Incorporations from Theodotion</span></h3>
<p>In most ancient copies of the Bible which contain the Septuagint version of the Old Testament, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Book of Daniel</a> is not the original Septuagint version, but instead is a copy of <a href="/wiki/Theodotion" title="Theodotion">Theodotion</a>'s translation from the Hebrew, which more closely resembles the Masoretic Text.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Septuagint version was discarded in favour of Theodotion's version in the 2nd to 3rd centuries CE. In Greek-speaking areas, this happened near the end of the 2nd century, and in Latin-speaking areas (at least in North Africa), it occurred in the middle of the 3rd century. History does not record the reason for this, and St. <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> reports, in the preface to the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a> version of Daniel, "This thing 'just' happened."<sup id="cite_ref-EncBibTV_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncBibTV-54">[54]</a></sup> One of two Old Greek texts of the Book of Daniel has been recently rediscovered and work is ongoing in reconstructing the original form of the book.<sup id="cite_ref-Dines_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dines-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>The canonical <a href="/wiki/Ezra%E2%80%93Nehemiah" title="Ezra–Nehemiah">Ezra–Nehemiah</a> is known in the Septuagint as "Esdras B", and 1 Esdras is "Esdras A". 1 Esdras is a very similar text to the books of Ezra–Nehemiah, and the two are widely thought by scholars to be derived from the same original text. It has been proposed, and is thought highly likely by scholars, that "Esdras B" – the canonical Ezra–Nehemiah – is Theodotion's version of this material, and "Esdras A" is the version which was previously in the Septuagint on its own.<sup id="cite_ref-EncBibTV_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncBibTV-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_form">Final form</span></h3>
<p>Some texts are found in the Septuagint but are not present in the Hebrew. These additional books are <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Tobit" title="Book of Tobit">Tobit</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judith" class="mw-redirect" title="Judith">Judith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Wisdom" title="Book of Wisdom">Wisdom of Solomon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sirach" title="Sirach">Wisdom of Jesus son of Sirach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Baruch" title="Book of Baruch">Baruch</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Letter_of_Jeremiah" title="Letter of Jeremiah">Letter of Jeremiah</a> (which later became chapter 6 of Baruch in the Vulgate), <a href="/wiki/Additions_to_Daniel" title="Additions to Daniel">additions to Daniel</a> (<a href="/wiki/The_Prayer_of_Azariah" class="mw-redirect" title="The Prayer of Azariah">The Prayer of Azarias</a>, the <a href="/wiki/The_Prayer_of_Azariah_and_Song_of_the_Three_Holy_Children" title="The Prayer of Azariah and Song of the Three Holy Children">Song of the Three Children</a>, <a href="/wiki/Susanna_(Book_of_Daniel)" title="Susanna (Book of Daniel)">Susanna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bel_and_the_Dragon" title="Bel and the Dragon">Bel and the Dragon</a>), additions to <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Esther" title="Book of Esther">Esther</a>, <a href="/wiki/1_Maccabees" title="1 Maccabees">1 Maccabees</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_Maccabees" title="2 Maccabees">2 Maccabees</a>, <a href="/wiki/3_Maccabees" title="3 Maccabees">3 Maccabees</a>, <a href="/wiki/4_Maccabees" title="4 Maccabees">4 Maccabees</a>, <a href="/wiki/1_Esdras" title="1 Esdras">1 Esdras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Odes_(Bible)" title="Book of Odes (Bible)">Odes</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Prayer_of_Manasseh" title="Prayer of Manasseh">Prayer of Manasseh</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Psalms_of_Solomon" title="Psalms of Solomon">Psalms of Solomon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Psalm_151" title="Psalm 151">Psalm 151</a>.</p>
<p>Some books that are set apart in the Masoretic Text are grouped together. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Samuel" title="Books of Samuel">Books of Samuel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Kings" title="Books of Kings">Books of Kings</a> are in the LXX one book in four parts called Βασιλειῶν ("Of Reigns"). In LXX, the <a href="/wiki/Books_of_Chronicles" title="Books of Chronicles">Books of Chronicles</a> supplement Reigns and it is called <i>Paralipomenon</i> (Παραλειπομένων – things left out). The Septuagint organizes the <a href="/wiki/Minor_prophets" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor prophets">minor prophets</a> as twelve parts of one Book of Twelve.<sup id="cite_ref-Dines_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dines-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
