<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Christian_biblical_canons" title="Christian biblical canons">Christian biblical canons</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_English_Bible_translations" title="List of English Bible translations">List of English Bible translations</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gutenberg_Bible_scan.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Gutenberg_Bible_scan.jpg/220px-Gutenberg_Bible_scan.jpg" width="220" height="279" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/Gutenberg_Bible_scan.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="297" data-file-height="376" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gutenberg_Bible_scan.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A page from the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">Gutenberg Bible</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A Christian Bible is a set of books that a <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denomination</a> regards as <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">divinely inspired</a> and thus constituting <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">scripture</a>. Although the <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Church</a> primarily used the Septuagint or the <a href="/wiki/Targum" title="Targum">Targums</a> among <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> speakers, the <a href="/wiki/Apostle_(Christian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Apostle (Christian)">apostles</a> did not leave a defined set of new scriptures; instead the canon of the New Testament <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">developed over time</a>. Groups within Christianity include differing books as part of their sacred writings, most prominent among which are the biblical apocrypha or deuterocanonical books.</p>
<p>Significant versions of the English Christian Bible include the <a href="/wiki/Douay-Rheims_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="Douay-Rheims Bible">Douay-Rheims Bible</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorized King James Version">Authorized King James Version</a>, the <a href="/wiki/English_Revised_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="English Revised Version">English Revised Version</a>, the <a href="/wiki/American_Standard_Version" title="American Standard Version">American Standard Version</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Revised_Standard_Version" title="Revised Standard Version">Revised Standard Version</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_American_Standard_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="New American Standard Version">New American Standard Version</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_King_James_Version" title="New King James Version">New King James Version</a>, the <a href="/wiki/New_International_Version" title="New International Version">New International Version</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/English_Standard_Version" title="English Standard Version">English Standard Version</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Testament">Old Testament</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">Old Testament</a></div>
<p>The books which make up the Christian Old Testament differ between the Catholic (see <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Bible" title="Catholic Bible">Catholic Bible</a>), Orthodox, and Protestant (see <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Bible" title="Protestant Bible">Protestant Bible</a>) churches, with the Protestant movement accepting only those books contained in the Hebrew Bible, while Catholics and Orthodox have wider canons. A few groups consider particular translations to be divinely inspired, notably the Greek Septuagint and the Aramaic <a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Apocryphal_or_deuterocanonical_books">Apocryphal or deuterocanonical books</span></h4>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Christianity" title="Eastern Christianity">Eastern Christianity</a>, translations based on the Septuagint still prevail. The Septuagint was generally abandoned in favour of the 10th-century Masoretic Text as the basis for translations of the Old Testament into <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western</a> languages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Some modern Western translations since the <a href="/wiki/Wycliffe%27s_Bible" title="Wycliffe's Bible">14th century</a> make use of the Septuagint to clarify passages in the Masoretic Text, where the Septuagint may preserve a variant reading of the Hebrew text.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> They also sometimes adopt variants that appear in other texts, e.g., those discovered among the <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea_Scrolls" title="Dead Sea Scrolls">Dead Sea Scrolls</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>A number of books which are part of the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_versions_of_the_Bible" title="Syriac versions of the Bible">Peshitta</a> or the Greek Septuagint but are not found in the Hebrew (Rabbinic) Bible (i.e., among the protocanonical books) are often referred to as deuterocanonical books by Roman Catholics referring to a later secondary (i.e., deutero) canon, that canon as fixed definitively by the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> 1545–1563.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[61]</a></sup> It includes 46 books for the Old Testament (45 if Jeremiah and Lamentations are counted as one) and 27 for the New.<sup id="cite_ref-120__68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120_-68">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most Protestants term these books as <a href="/wiki/Biblical_apocrypha" title="Biblical apocrypha">apocrypha</a>. Modern <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestant</a> traditions do not accept the deuterocanonical books as canonical, although Protestant Bibles included them in Apocrypha sections until the 1820s. However, Roman Catholic and Eastern Orthodox Churches include these books as part of their Old Testament.</p>
<p>The Roman Catholic Church recognizes:<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[63]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Tobit" title="Book of Tobit">Tobit</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Judith" title="Book of Judith">Judith</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/1_Maccabees" title="1 Maccabees">1 Maccabees</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/2_Maccabees" title="2 Maccabees">2 Maccabees</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Wisdom" title="Book of Wisdom">Wisdom</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wisdom_of_Sirach" class="mw-redirect" title="Wisdom of Sirach">Sirach</a> (or Ecclesiasticus)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Baruch" title="Book of Baruch">Baruch</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Letter_of_Jeremiah" class="mw-redirect" title="The Letter of Jeremiah">The Letter of Jeremiah</a> (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Baruch" title="Book of Baruch">Baruch</a> Chapter 6)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Additions_to_Esther" class="mw-redirect" title="Additions to Esther">Greek Additions to Esther</a> (Book of Esther, chapters 10:4–12:6)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/The_Prayer_of_Azariah_and_Song_of_the_Three_Holy_Children" title="The Prayer of Azariah and Song of the Three Holy Children">The Prayer of Azariah and Song of the Three Holy Children</a> <i>verses 1–68</i> (Book of Daniel, chapter 3, verses 24–90)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Susanna_(Book_of_Daniel)" title="Susanna (Book of Daniel)">Susanna</a> (Book of Daniel, chapter 13)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bel_and_the_Dragon" title="Bel and the Dragon">Bel and the Dragon</a> (Book of Daniel, chapter 14)</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition to those, the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Orthodox_Church" title="Greek Orthodox Church">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Churches</a> recognize the following:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/3_Maccabees" title="3 Maccabees">3 Maccabees</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/1_Esdras" title="1 Esdras">1 Esdras</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Prayer_of_Manasseh" title="Prayer of Manasseh">Prayer of Manasseh</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Psalm_151" title="Psalm 151">Psalm 151</a></li>
</ul>
<p>Russian and Georgian Orthodox Churches include:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/2_Esdras" title="2 Esdras">2 Esdras</a> i.e., Latin Esdras in the Russian and Georgian Bibles</li>
</ul>
<p>There is also <a href="/wiki/4_Maccabees" title="4 Maccabees">4 Maccabees</a> which is only accepted as canonical in the <a href="/wiki/Georgian_Orthodox_Church" title="Georgian Orthodox Church">Georgian Church</a>, but was included by <a href="/wiki/St._Jerome" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Jerome">St. Jerome</a> in an appendix to the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>, and is an appendix to the Greek Orthodox Bible, and it is therefore sometimes included in collections of the Apocrypha.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Orthodox_Church" title="Syriac Orthodox Church">Syriac Orthodox tradition</a> includes:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Psalms_152%E2%80%93155" title="Psalms 152–155">Psalms 151–155</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Apocalypse_of_Baruch" class="mw-redirect" title="Syriac Apocalypse of Baruch">Apocalypse of Baruch</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/2_Baruch#The_Letter_of_Baruch" title="2 Baruch">The Letter of Baruch</a></li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Biblical_canon" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopian Biblical canon">Ethiopian Biblical canon</a> includes:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jubilees" class="mw-redirect" title="Jubilees">Jubilees</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Enoch" title="Book of Enoch">Enoch</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Meqabyan" title="Meqabyan">1–3 Meqabyan</a></li>
</ul>
<p>and some other books.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a> uses some of the <a href="/wiki/Deuterocanonical_books" title="Deuterocanonical books">Apocryphal books</a> liturgically. Therefore, editions of the Bible intended for use in the Anglican Church include the Deuterocanonical books accepted by the Catholic Church, plus <a href="/wiki/1_Esdras" title="1 Esdras">1 Esdras</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_Esdras" title="2 Esdras">2 Esdras</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Prayer_of_Manasseh" title="Prayer of Manasseh">Prayer of Manasseh</a>, which were in the Vulgate appendix.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pseudepigraphal_books">Pseudepigraphal books</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pseudepigrapha" title="Pseudepigrapha">Pseudepigrapha</a></div>
<p>The term Pseudepigrapha commonly describes numerous works of Jewish religious literature written from about 300 BCE to 300 CE. Not all of these works are actually pseudepigraphical. It also refers to books of the New Testament canon whose authorship is misrepresented. The "Old Testament" Pseudepigraphal works include the following:<sup id="cite_ref-Harris_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-70">[64]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/3_Maccabees" title="3 Maccabees">3 Maccabees</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/4_Maccabees" title="4 Maccabees">4 Maccabees</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Assumption_of_Moses" title="Assumption of Moses">Assumption of Moses</a></li>
<li>Ethiopic <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Enoch" title="Book of Enoch">Book of Enoch</a> (1 Enoch)</li>
<li>Slavonic <a href="/wiki/Second_Book_of_Enoch" title="Second Book of Enoch">Book of Enoch</a> (2 Enoch)</li>
<li>Hebrew <a href="/wiki/3_Enoch" title="3 Enoch">Book of Enoch</a> (3 Enoch) (also known as "The Revelation of Metatron" or "The Book of Rabbi Ishmael the High Priest")</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Jubilees" title="Book of Jubilees">Book of Jubilees</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Syriac_Apocalypse_of_Baruch" class="mw-redirect" title="Syriac Apocalypse of Baruch">Syriac Apocalypse of Baruch</a> (2 Baruch)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Letter_of_Aristeas" title="Letter of Aristeas">Letter of Aristeas</a> (Letter to Philocrates regarding the translating of the Hebrew Scriptures into Greek)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Life_of_Adam_and_Eve" title="Life of Adam and Eve">Life of Adam and Eve</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Martyrdom_and_Ascension_of_Isaiah" class="mw-redirect" title="Martyrdom and Ascension of Isaiah">Martyrdom and Ascension of Isaiah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Psalms_of_Solomon" title="Psalms of Solomon">Psalms of Solomon</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sibylline_Oracles" title="Sibylline Oracles">Sibylline Oracles</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greek_Apocalypse_of_Baruch" class="mw-redirect" title="Greek Apocalypse of Baruch">Greek Apocalypse of Baruch</a> (3 Baruch)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Testaments_of_the_Twelve_Patriarchs" title="Testaments of the Twelve Patriarchs">Testaments of the Twelve Patriarchs</a></li>
</ul>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Book_of_Enoch">Book of Enoch</span></h5>
<p>Notable pseudepigraphal works include the Books of Enoch (such as <a href="/wiki/1_Enoch" class="mw-redirect" title="1 Enoch">1 Enoch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Second_Book_of_Enoch" title="Second Book of Enoch">2 Enoch</a>, surviving only in <a href="/wiki/Old_Church_Slavonic" title="Old Church Slavonic">Old Slavonic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/3_Enoch" title="3 Enoch">3 Enoch</a>, surviving in <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, c. 5th to 6th century CE). These are ancient <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> religious works, traditionally ascribed to the prophet <a href="/wiki/Enoch_(Biblical_figure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Enoch (Biblical figure)">Enoch</a>, the great-grandfather of the patriarch <a href="/wiki/Noah" title="Noah">Noah</a>. They are not part of the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">biblical canon</a> used by <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a>, apart from <a href="/wiki/Beta_Israel" title="Beta Israel">Beta Israel</a>. Most Christian denominations and traditions may accept the Books of Enoch as having some historical or theological interest or significance. It has been observed that part of the Book of Enoch is quoted in the <a href="/wiki/Epistle_of_Jude" title="Epistle of Jude">Epistle of Jude</a> (part of the New Testament) but Christian denominations generally regard the Books of Enoch as non-canonical or non-inspired.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[65]</a></sup> However, the Enoch books are treated as canonical by the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eritrean_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Eritrean Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Eritrean Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a>.</p>
<p>The older sections (mainly in the Book of the Watchers) are estimated to date from about 300&#160;BC, and the latest part (Book of Parables) probably was composed at the end of the 1st century&#160;BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Denominational_views_of_Pseudepigrapha">Denominational views of Pseudepigrapha</span></h5>
<p>There arose in some Protestant biblical scholarship an extended use of the term <i>pseudepigrapha</i> for works that appeared as though they ought to be part of the biblical canon, because of the authorship ascribed to them, but which stood outside both the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">biblical canons</a> recognized by Protestants and Catholics. These works were also outside the particular set of books that Roman Catholics called <i>deuterocanonical</i> and to which Protestants had generally applied the term Apocryphal. Accordingly, the term <i>pseudepigraphical</i>, as now used often among both Protestants and Roman Catholics (allegedly for the clarity it brings to the discussion), may make it difficult to discuss questions of pseudepigraphical authorship of canonical books dispassionately with a lay audience. To confuse the matter even more, Eastern Orthodox Christians accept books as canonical that Roman Catholics and most Protestant denominations consider pseudepigraphical or at best of much less authority. There exist also churches that reject some of the books that Roman Catholics, Orthodox and Protestants accept. The same is true of some <a href="/wiki/Jewish_denominations" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish denominations">Jewish sects</a>. Many works that are "apocryphal" are otherwise considered genuine.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Role_of_the_Old_Testament_in_Christian_theology">Role of the Old Testament in Christian theology</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">Sola scriptura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a></div>
<p>The Old Testament has always been central to the life of the Christian church. Bible scholar <a href="/wiki/N.T._Wright" class="mw-redirect" title="N.T. Wright">N.T. Wright</a> says "Jesus himself was profoundly shaped by the scriptures."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWright20053_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWright20053-73">[67]</a></sup> He adds that the earliest Christians also searched those same Hebrew scriptures in their effort to understand the earthly life of Jesus. They regarded the "holy writings" of the Israelites as necessary and instructive for the Christian, as seen from Paul's words to Timothy (2 Timothy 3:15), and as pointing to the Messiah, and as having reached a climactic fulfillment in Jesus himself, generating the "<a href="/wiki/New_covenant" class="mw-redirect" title="New covenant">new covenant</a>" prophesied by <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah" title="Jeremiah">Jeremiah</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Wright_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-74">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Testament">New Testament</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">Development of the New Testament canon</a></div>
<p>The New Testament is the name given to the second and final portion of the Christian Bible. <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a> is its central figure.</p>
<p>The term "New Testament" came into use in the second century during a controversy among Christians over whether or not the Hebrew Bible should be included with the Christian writings as sacred scripture. The New Testament presupposes the inspiration of the Old Testament.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[69]</a></sup> Some other works which were widely read by early churches were excluded from the New Testament and relegated to the collections known as the <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Fathers" title="Apostolic Fathers">Apostolic Fathers</a> (generally considered orthodox) and the New Testament <a href="/wiki/Apocrypha" title="Apocrypha">Apocrypha</a> (including both orthodox and heretical works). Most Christians consider the New Testament to be an infallible source of doctrine, while others go even farther to affirm that it is also inerrant, or completely correct in historical and factual details as well as theologically. In recent times, however, the authority of the New Testament books has been challenged. The school of historical criticism has exposed various apparent contradictions within the texts, as well as questions of authorship and dating.</p>
<p>The New Testament is a collection of 27 books<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[70]</a></sup> of 4 different <a href="/wiki/Genres" class="mw-redirect" title="Genres">genres</a> of Christian literature (<a href="/wiki/Gospels" class="mw-redirect" title="Gospels">Gospels</a>, one account of the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts of the Apostles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epistles" class="mw-redirect" title="Epistles">Epistles</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Apocalyptic_literature" title="Apocalyptic literature">Apocalypse</a>). These books can be grouped into:</p>
<div>
<table class="multicol" role="presentation" style="border-collapse: collapse; padding: 0; border: 0; background:transparent; width:100%;">
<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; vertical-align: top;">
<p><a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">The Gospels</a></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Synoptic_Gospels" title="Synoptic Gospels">Synoptic Gospels</a>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Matthew" title="Gospel of Matthew">Gospel According to Matthew</a></li>

</div>
<p>The New Testament books are ordered differently in the Catholic/Orthodox/Protestant tradition, the <a href="/wiki/Slavonic_translations_of_the_Bible" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavonic translations of the Bible">Slavonic tradition</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_versions_of_the_Bible" title="Syriac versions of the Bible">Syriac</a> tradition and the Ethiopian tradition.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Original_language">Original language</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Language_of_the_New_Testament" title="Language of the New Testament">Language of the New Testament</a></div>
<p>The mainstream consensus is that the New Testament was written in a form of <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[72]</a></sup> which was the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">common language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[73]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[76]</a></sup> from the <a href="/wiki/Conquests_of_Alexander_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Conquests of Alexander the Great">Conquests of Alexander the Great</a> (335–323 BCE) until the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greek" class="mw-redirect" title="Byzantine Greek">Byzantine Greek</a> (c. 600).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Historic_editions">Historic editions</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Biblical_manuscript" title="Biblical manuscript">Biblical manuscript</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">Textual criticism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/220px-Lutherbibel.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/330px-Lutherbibel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Lutherbibel.jpg/440px-Lutherbibel.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="759" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lutherbibel.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An early German translation by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">Martin Luther</a>. His translation of the text into the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">vernacular</a> was highly influential.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The original <a href="/wiki/Autograph" title="Autograph">autographs</a>, that is, the original Greek writings and <a href="/wiki/Biblical_manuscript" title="Biblical manuscript">manuscripts</a> written by the original authors of the New Testament, have not survived.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[77]</a></sup> But historically <i>copies</i> exist of those original autographs, transmitted and preserved in a number of <a href="/wiki/Bible_manuscript#Textual_criticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bible manuscript">manuscript traditions</a>. There have been some minor variations, additions or omissions, in some of the texts. When ancient scribes copied earlier books, they sometimes wrote notes on the margins of the page (<i><a href="/wiki/Glosses_to_the_Bible#Glosses_as_marginal_notes" class="mw-redirect" title="Glosses to the Bible">marginal glosses</a></i>) to correct their text – especially if a scribe accidentally omitted a word or line – and to comment about the text. When later scribes were copying the copy, they were sometimes uncertain if a note was intended to be included as part of the text. Over time, different regions evolved different versions, each with its own assemblage of omissions and additions.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The three main textual traditions of the Greek New Testament are sometimes called the <a href="/wiki/Alexandrian_text-type" title="Alexandrian text-type">Alexandrian text-type</a> (generally minimalist), the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_text-type" title="Byzantine text-type">Byzantine text-type</a> (generally maximalist), and the <a href="/wiki/Western_text-type" title="Western text-type">Western text-type</a> (occasionally wild). Together they comprise most of the ancient manuscripts.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_the_Christian_canons">Development of the Christian canons</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_Old_Testament_canon" title="Development of the Old Testament canon">Development of the Old Testament canon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Development_of_the_New_Testament_canon" title="Development of the New Testament canon">Development of the New Testament canon</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Marinus_Claesz._van_Reymerswaele_002.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Marinus_Claesz._van_Reymerswaele_002.jpg/220px-Marinus_Claesz._van_Reymerswaele_002.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Marinus_Claesz._van_Reymerswaele_002.jpg/330px-Marinus_Claesz._van_Reymerswaele_002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Marinus_Claesz._van_Reymerswaele_002.jpg/440px-Marinus_Claesz._van_Reymerswaele_002.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3051" data-file-height="2311" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Marinus_Claesz._van_Reymerswaele_002.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> (pictured) produced a 4th-century <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> edition of the Bible, known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a></i>, that became the Catholic Church's official translation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Old Testament canon entered into Christian use in the Greek Septuagint translations and original books, and their differing lists of texts. In addition to the Septuagint, Christianity<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (December 2014)">vague</span></a></i>]</sup> subsequently added various writings that would become the New Testament. Somewhat different lists of accepted works continued to develop in antiquity. In the 4th century a series of <a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synods</a> produced a list of texts equal to the 39, 46, 51, or 54-book canon of the Old Testament and to the 27-book canon of the New Testament that would be subsequently used to today, most notably the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Hippo" title="Synod of Hippo">Synod of Hippo</a> in 393 CE. Also <i>c</i>. 400, <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> produced a definitive Latin edition of the Bible (see <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>), the canon of which, at the insistence of the Pope, was in accord with the earlier Synods. With the benefit of hindsight it can be said that this process effectively set the New Testament canon, although there are examples of other canonical lists in use after this time.</p>
<p>The Protestant Old Testament of today has a 39-book canon – the number of books (though not the content) varies from the Jewish Tanakh only because of a different method of division – while the Roman Catholic Church recognizes 46 books (51 books with some books combined into 46 books) as the canonical Old Testament. The Eastern Orthodox Churches recognize 3 Maccabees, 1 Esdras, Prayer of Manasseh and Psalm 151 in addition to the Catholic canon. Some include 2 Esdras. The Anglican Church also recognizes a longer canon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The term "Hebrew Scriptures" is often used as being synonymous with the Protestant Old Testament, since the surviving scriptures in Hebrew include only those books, while Catholics and Orthodox include additional texts that have not survived in Hebrew. Both Catholics and Protestants (as well as Greek Orthodox) have the same 27-book New Testament Canon.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The New Testament writers assumed the inspiration of the Old Testament, probably earliest stated in <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2Tim+3%3A16&amp;version=9">2 Timothy 3:16</a></span>, "All scripture is given by inspiration of God".<sup id="cite_ref-Stagg_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stagg-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethiopian_Orthodox_canon">Ethiopian Orthodox canon</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Biblical_canon" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethiopian Biblical canon">Ethiopian Biblical canon</a></div>
<p>The Canon of the <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Orthodox_Tewahedo_Church" title="Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church">Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church</a> is wider than the canons used by most other Christian churches. There are 81 books in the Ethiopian Orthodox Bible.<sup id="cite_ref-Ethiopian_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ethiopian-86">[80]</a></sup> The Ethiopian Old Testament Canon includes the books found in the <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">Septuagint</a> accepted by other Orthodox Christians, in addition to <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Enoch" title="Book of Enoch">Enoch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jubilees" class="mw-redirect" title="Jubilees">Jubilees</a> which are ancient Jewish books that only survived in <a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ge'ez language">Ge'ez</a> but are quoted in the New Testament,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> also Greek Ezra <a href="/wiki/1_Esdras" title="1 Esdras">First</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_Apocalypse_of_Ezra" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish Apocalypse of Ezra">Apocalypse of Ezra</a>, 3 books of <a href="/wiki/Meqabyan" title="Meqabyan">Meqabyan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Psalm_151" title="Psalm 151">Psalm 151</a> at the end of the Psalter. The three books of Meqabyan are not to be confused with the books of Maccabees. The order of the other books is somewhat different from other groups', as well. The Old Testament follows the Septuagint order for the Minor Prophets rather than the Jewish order.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Divine_inspiration">Divine inspiration</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">Biblical inspiration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biblical_literalism" title="Biblical literalism">Biblical literalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biblical_infallibility" title="Biblical infallibility">Biblical infallibility</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">Biblical inerrancy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Altar_and_bible_st_Johns_Lutheran.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Altar_and_bible_st_Johns_Lutheran.jpg/220px-Altar_and_bible_st_Johns_Lutheran.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Altar_and_bible_st_Johns_Lutheran.jpg/330px-Altar_and_bible_st_Johns_Lutheran.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Altar_and_bible_st_Johns_Lutheran.jpg/440px-Altar_and_bible_st_Johns_Lutheran.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Altar_and_bible_st_Johns_Lutheran.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Bible is placed centrally on a <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> altar, highlighting its importance</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Second Epistle to Timothy says that "all scripture is given by inspiration of God, and is profitable for doctrine, for reproof, for correction, for instruction in righteousness". (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=2+Timothy+3%3A16&amp;version=KJV">2 Timothy 3:16</a></span>)<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[81]</a></sup> Various related but distinguishable views on divine inspiration include:</p>
<ul>
<li>the view of the Bible as the inspired word of God: the belief that God, through the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit" title="Holy Spirit">Holy Spirit</a>, intervened and influenced the words, message, and collation of the Bible<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li>the view that the Bible is also <a href="/wiki/Biblical_infallibility" title="Biblical infallibility">infallible</a>, and incapable of error in matters of faith and practice, but not necessarily in historic or scientific matters</li>
<li>the view that the Bible represents the <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">inerrant word of God</a>, without error in any aspect, spoken by God and written down in its perfect form by humans</li>
</ul>
<p>Within these broad beliefs many schools of hermeneutics operate. "Bible scholars claim that discussions about the Bible must be put into its context within church history and then into the context of contemporary culture."<sup id="cite_ref-Wright_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wright-74">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christian_fundamentalism" title="Christian fundamentalism">Fundamentalist Christians</a> are associated<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> with the doctrine of biblical literalism, where the Bible is not only inerrant, but the meaning of the text is clear to the average reader.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jewish antiquity attests to belief in sacred texts,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Josephus_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Josephus-91">[85]</a></sup> and a similar belief emerges in the earliest of Christian writings. Various texts of the Bible mention divine agency in relation to its writings.<sup id="cite_ref-Original_Source_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Original_Source-92">[86]</a></sup> In their book <i>A General Introduction to the Bible</i>, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Geisler" title="Norman Geisler">Norman Geisler</a> and William Nix write: "The process of inspiration is a mystery of the providence of God, but the result of this process is a verbal, plenary, inerrant, and authoritative record."<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup> Most evangelical biblical scholars<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup> associate inspiration with only the original text; for example some American Protestants adhere to the 1978 <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Statement_on_Biblical_Inerrancy" title="Chicago Statement on Biblical Inerrancy">Chicago Statement on Biblical Inerrancy</a> which asserted that inspiration applied only to the <a href="/wiki/Autograph" title="Autograph">autographic</a> text of Scripture.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup> Among adherents of Biblical literalism, a minority, such as followers of the <a href="/wiki/King-James-Only_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="King-James-Only Movement">King-James-Only Movement</a>, extend the claim of inerrancy only to a particular translation.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Versions_and_translations">Versions and translations</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations" title="Bible translations">Bible translations</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Bible_translations_by_language" title="List of Bible translations by language">List of Bible translations by language</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg/220px-1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg" width="220" height="351" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg/330px-1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg/440px-1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg 2x" data-file-width="753" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1588_First_Welsh_Bible.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Title page from the first <a href="/wiki/Welsh_language" title="Welsh language">Welsh</a> translation of the Bible, 1588. <a href="/wiki/William_Morgan_(Bible_translator)" title="William Morgan (Bible translator)">William Morgan</a> (1545–1604)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The original texts of the Tanakh were mainly in Hebrew, with some portions in Aramaic. In addition to the authoritative Masoretic Text, Jews still refer to the Septuagint, the translation of the Hebrew Bible into Greek, and the <a href="/wiki/Targum_Onkelos" title="Targum Onkelos">Targum Onkelos</a>, an Aramaic version of the Bible. There are several different ancient versions of the Tanakh in Hebrew, mostly differing by spelling, and the traditional Jewish version is based on the version known as Aleppo Codex. Even in this version there are words which are traditionally read differently from written, because the oral tradition is considered more fundamental than the written one, and presumably mistakes had been made in copying the text over the generations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The primary biblical text for early Christians was the Septuagint. In addition, they translated the Hebrew Bible into several other languages. Translations were made into Syriac, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">Coptic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ge'ez language">Ethiopic</a>, and Latin, among other languages. The Latin translations were historically the most important for the Church in the West, while the Greek-speaking East continued to use the Septuagint translations of the Old Testament and had no need to translate the New Testament.</p>
<p>The earliest Latin translation was the <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a> text, or <i><a href="/wiki/Vetus_Latina" title="Vetus Latina">Vetus Latina</a></i>, which, from internal evidence, seems to have been made by several authors over a period of time. It was based on the Septuagint, and thus included books not in the Hebrew Bible.</p>
<p>According to the Latin <a href="/wiki/Decretum_Gelasianum" title="Decretum Gelasianum">Decretum Gelasianum</a> (also known as the Gelasian Decree), thought to be of a 6th-century document<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce1988234_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce1988234-100">[94]</a></sup> of uncertain authorship and of pseudepigraphal papal authority (variously ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gelasius_I" title="Pope Gelasius I">Pope Gelasius I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Damasus_I" title="Pope Damasus I">Pope Damasus I</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Pope_Hormisdas" title="Pope Hormisdas">Pope Hormisdas</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> but reflecting the views of the Roman Church by that period,<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Rome" title="Council of Rome">Council of Rome</a> in 382 AD under Pope Damasus I (366–383) assembled a list of books of the Bible. Damasus commissioned Saint <a href="/wiki/Jerome" title="Jerome">Jerome</a> to produce a reliable and consistent text by translating the original Greek and Hebrew texts into Latin. This translation became known as the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Latin Vulgate Bible</a>, in the fourth century AD (although Jerome expressed in his prologues to most <a href="/wiki/Biblical_apocrypha" title="Biblical apocrypha"><i>deuterocanonical</i></a> books that they were non-<a href="/wiki/Biblical_canon" title="Biblical canon">canonical</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> In 1546, at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a>, Jerome's Vulgate translation was declared by the Roman Catholic Church to be the only authentic and official Bible in the <a href="/wiki/Latin_Church" title="Latin Church">Latin Church</a>.</p>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations" title="Bible translations">Bible translations</a> for many languages have been made. The Bible continues to be translated to new languages, largely by Christian organizations such as <a href="/wiki/Wycliffe_Bible_Translators" class="mw-redirect" title="Wycliffe Bible Translators">Wycliffe Bible Translators</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Tribes_Mission" title="New Tribes Mission">New Tribes Mission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bible_society" title="Bible society">Bible societies</a>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Views">Views</span></h2>
<p>John Riches, professor of Divinity and Biblical Criticism at the University of Glasgow, provides the following view of the diverse historical influences of the Bible:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>It has inspired some of the great monuments of human thought, literature, and art; it has equally fuelled some of the worst excesses of human savagery, self-interest, and narrow-mindedness. It has inspired men and women to acts of great service and courage, to fight for liberation and human development; and it has provided the ideological fuel for societies which have enslaved their fellow human beings and reduced them to abject poverty. ... It has, perhaps above all, provided a source of religious and moral norms which have enabled communities to hold together, to care for, and to protect one another; yet precisely this strong sense of belonging has in turn fuelled ethnic, racial, and international tension and conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiches2000134_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiches2000134-108">[102]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_religions">Other religions</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_the_Christian_Bible" title="Islamic view of the Christian Bible">Islamic view of the Christian Bible</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, the Bible is held to reflect true unfolding <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a> from <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">God</a>; but revelation which had been corrupted or distorted (in Arabic: <i><a href="/wiki/Tahrif" title="Tahrif">tahrif</a></i>); which necessitated the giving of the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" class="mw-redirect" title="Qur'an">Qur'an</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Prophets_of_Islam" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophets of Islam">Islamic prophet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">Muhammad</a>, to correct this deviation.</p>
<p>Members of other religions may also seek inspiration from the Bible. For example, <a href="/wiki/Rastafari" title="Rastafari">Rastafaris</a> view the Bible as essential to their religion<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalists</a> view it as "one of many important religious texts".<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_studies">Biblical studies</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biblical_studies" title="Biblical studies">Biblical studies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">Biblical criticism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">Biblical criticism</a> refers to the investigation of the Bible as a text, and addresses questions such as authorship, dates of composition, and authorial intention. It is not the same as <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Bible" title="Criticism of the Bible">criticism of the Bible</a>, which is an assertion against the Bible being a source of information or ethical guidance, or observations that the Bible may have <a href="/wiki/Translation" title="Translation">translation</a> errors.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_criticism">Higher criticism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Higher_criticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Higher criticism">Higher criticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lower_criticism" class="mw-redirect" title="Lower criticism">Lower criticism</a></div>
<p>In the 17th century <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> collected the current evidence to conclude outright that Moses could not have written the bulk of the Torah. Shortly afterwards the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a> published a unified critical analysis, arguing that the problematic passages were not isolated cases that could be explained away one by one, but pervasive throughout the five books, concluding that it was "clearer than the sun at noon that the Pentateuch was not written by Moses . . ."<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Archaeological_and_historical_research">Archaeological and historical research</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Biblical_archaeology_school" title="Biblical archaeology school">Biblical archaeology school</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Bible_and_history" class="mw-redirect" title="The Bible and history">The Bible and history</a></div>
<p>Biblical archaeology is the <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a> that relates to and sheds light upon the Hebrew Scriptures and the Christian Greek Scriptures (or the "New Testament"). It is used to help determine the lifestyle and practices of people living in biblical times. There are a wide range of interpretations in the field of biblical archaeology. One broad division includes <a href="/wiki/Biblical_maximalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical maximalism">biblical maximalism</a> which generally takes the view that most of the Old Testament or the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">Hebrew Bible</a> is based on history although it is presented through the religious viewpoint of its time. It is considered to be the opposite of <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Minimalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Biblical Minimalism">biblical minimalism</a> which considers the Bible to be a purely <a href="/wiki/Post-exilic" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-exilic">post-exilic</a> (5th century BCE and later) composition. Even among those scholars who adhere to biblical minimalism, the Bible is a historical document containing first-hand information on the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman era">Roman eras</a>, and there is universal scholarly consensus that the events of the 6th century BCE <a href="/wiki/Babylonian_captivity" title="Babylonian captivity">Babylonian captivity</a> have a basis in history.</p>
<p>The historicity of the biblical account of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_ancient_Israel_and_Judah" title="History of ancient Israel and Judah">history of ancient Israel and Judah</a> of the 10th to 7th centuries BCE is disputed in scholarship. The biblical account of the 8th to 7th centuries BCE is widely, but not universally, accepted as historical, while the verdict on the earliest period of the <a href="/wiki/United_Monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="United Monarchy">United Monarchy</a> (10th century BCE) and the <a href="/wiki/Historicity_of_David" class="mw-redirect" title="Historicity of David">historicity of David</a> is unclear. Archaeological evidence providing information on this period, such as the <a href="/wiki/Tel_Dan_Stele" title="Tel Dan Stele">Tel Dan Stele</a>, can potentially be decisive. The biblical account of events of the <a href="/wiki/Exodus_from_Egypt" class="mw-redirect" title="Exodus from Egypt">Exodus from Egypt</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">Torah</a>, and the migration to the <a href="/wiki/Promised_Land" title="Promised Land">Promised Land</a> and the period of <a href="/wiki/Biblical_judges" title="Biblical judges">Judges</a> are not considered historical in scholarship.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Illustrations">Illustrations</span></h2>
<p>Most old Bibles were illuminated, they were <a href="/wiki/Manuscript" title="Manuscript">manuscripts</a> in which the <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">text</a> is supplemented by the addition of decoration, such as decorated <a href="/wiki/Initial" title="Initial">initials</a>, borders (<a href="/wiki/Marginalia" title="Marginalia">marginalia</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Miniature_(illuminated_manuscript)" title="Miniature (illuminated manuscript)">miniature illustrations</a>. Up to the twelfth century, most manuscripts were produced in monasteries in order to add to the library or after receiving a <a href="/wiki/Commission_(art)" title="Commission (art)">commission</a> from a wealthy patron. Larger monasteries often contained separate areas for the <a href="/wiki/Monks" class="mw-redirect" title="Monks">monks</a> who specialized in the production of manuscripts called a <a href="/wiki/Scriptorium" title="Scriptorium">scriptorium</a>, where "separate little rooms were assigned to book copying; they were situated in such a way that each scribe had to himself a window open to the cloister walk."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1962_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1962-116">[110]</a></sup> By the fourteenth century, the <a href="/wiki/Cloisters" class="mw-redirect" title="Cloisters">cloisters</a> of monks writing in the scriptorium started to employ laybrothers from the urban scriptoria, especially in Paris, Rome and the Netherlands.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Hamel199245_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Hamel199245-117">[111]</a></sup> Demand for manuscripts grew to an extent that the Monastic libraries were unable to meet with the demand, and began employing secular scribes and illuminators.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Hamel199257_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Hamel199257-118">[112]</a></sup> These individuals often lived close to the monastery and, in certain instances, dressed as monks whenever they entered the monastery, but were allowed to leave at the end of the day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Hamel199265_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Hamel199265-119">[113]</a></sup></p>
<p>The manuscript was "sent to the <a href="/wiki/Rubricator" class="mw-redirect" title="Rubricator">rubricator</a>, who added (in red or other colours) the titles, <a href="/wiki/Headlines" class="mw-redirect" title="Headlines">headlines</a>, the initials of chapters and sections, the notes and so on; and then – if the book was to be illustrated – it was sent to the illuminator."<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1962_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1962-116">[110]</a></sup> In the case of manuscripts that were sold commercially, the writing would "undoubtedly have been discussed initially between the patron and the scribe (or the scribe's agent,) but by the time that the written gathering were sent off to the illuminator there was no longer any scope for innovation."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDe_Hamel199260_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDe_Hamel199260-120">[114]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 1215px;_width: 1215px;">
<li class='gallerycaption'>Bible illustrations</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-57"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-57">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The canon of the original Old Greek LXX is disputed. This table reflects the canon of the Old Testament as used currently in Orthodoxy.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Βασιλειῶν</span> (Basileiōn) is the genitive plural of <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Βασιλεῖα</span> (Basileia).<br /></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">That is, <i>Things set aside</i> from <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">Ἔσδρας Αʹ</span>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-60"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-60">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Also called Τωβείτ or Τωβίθ in some sources.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-62"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-62">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Obdiou is genitive from "The vision <i>of</i> Obdias", which opens the book.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-63"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-63">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Originally placed after 3 Maccabees and before Psalms, but placed in an appendix of the Orthodox Canon</span></li>
</ol>

<li id="cite_note-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-28">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Gilbert Rosenthal 1973 <i>Four paths to One God</i> Bloch Publishing pp. 116–28, 180–92, 238–42</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1Kings.18%3A24%3B1Kings.18%3A37%E2%80%9339+9&amp;version=NRSV">1Kings.18:24;1Kings.18:37–39 9</a></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-30"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-30">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">George Savran "I and II Kings" in <i>The Literary Guide to the Bible</i> edited by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Alter" title="Robert Alter">Robert Alter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Kermode" title="Frank Kermode">Frank Kermode</a>. "Each king is judged either good or bad in black-and-white terms, according to whether or not he "did right" or "did evil" in the sight of the Lord. This evaluation is not reflective of the well-being of the nation, of the king's success or failure in war, or of the moral climate of the times, but rather the state of cultic worship during his reign. Those kings who shun idolatry and enact religious reforms are singled out for praise, and those who encourage pagan practices are denounced." 146</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Yehezkel_Kaufmann" title="Yehezkel Kaufmann">Yehezkel Kaufmann</a> "Israel In Canaan" in <i>Great Ages and Ideas of the Jewish People</i> edited by Leo Schwartz, The Modern Library. "The fight against Baal was initiated by the prophets" 54</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-32">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Yehezkel_Kaufmann" title="Yehezkel Kaufmann">Yehezkel Kaufmann</a> "The Age of Prophecy" in <i>Great Ages and Ideas of the Jewish People</i> edited by Leo Schwartz, The Modern Library. "The immediate occasion of the rise of the new prophecy was the political and social ruin caused by the wars with Israel's northerly neighbor, Aram, which continued for more than a century. They raged intensely during the reign of Ahab, and did not end until the time of Jeroboam II (784–744). While the nation as a whole was impoverished, a few&#160;– apparently of the royal officialdom&#160;– grew wealthy as a result of the national calamity. Many of the people were compelled to sell their houses and lands, with the result that a sharp social cleavage arose: on the one hand a mass of propertyless indigents, on the other a small circle of the rich. A series of disasters struck the nation&#160;– drought, famine, plagues, death and captivity (Amos 4: 6–11), but the greatest disaster of all was the social disintegration due to the cleavage between the poor masses and the wealthy, dissolute upper class. The decay affected both Judah and Israel ... High minded men were appalled at this development. Was this the people whom YHWH had brought out of Egypt, to whom He had given the land and a law of justice and right? it seemed as if the land was about to be inherited by the rich, who would squander its substance in drunken revelry. it was this dissolution that brought the prophetic denunciations to white heat." 57–58</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Abraham_Joshua_Heschel" title="Abraham Joshua Heschel">Abraham Joshua Heschel</a> 1955 <i>The Prophets</i> Harper and Row: "What manner of man is the prophet? A student of philosophy who runs from the discourses of the great metaphysicians to the orations of the prophets may feel as if he were going from the realm of the sublime to an area of trivialities. Instead of dealing with the timeless issues of being and becoming, of matter and form, of definitions and demonstrations, he is thrown into orations about widows and orphans, about the corruption of judges and affairs of the market place. Instead of showing us a way through the elegant mansions of the mind, the prophets take us to the slums. The world is a proud place, full of beauty, but the prophets are scandalized, and rave as if the whole world were a slum. They make much ado about paltry things, lavishing excessive language upon trifling subjects. What if somewhere in ancient Palestine poor people have not been treated properly by the rich? .... Indeed, the sorts of crimes and even the amount of delinquency that fill the prophets of Israel with dismay do not go beyond that which we regard as normal, as typical ingredients of social dynamics. To us a single act of injustice&#160;– cheating in business, exploitation of the poor – is slight; to the prophets, a disaster. To us an injustice is injurious to the welfare of the people; to the prophets it is a deathblow to existence; to us an episode; to them, a catastrophe, a threat to the world." 3–4</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Joel Rosenberg "I and II Samuel" in <i>The Literary Guide to the Bible</i> edited by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Alter" title="Robert Alter">Robert Alter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Kermode" title="Frank Kermode">Frank Kermode</a>. "Samuel is thus a work of national self-criticism. It recognizes that Israel would not have survived, either politically or culturally, without the steadying presence of a dynastic royal house. But it makes both that house and its subjects answerable to firm standards of prophetic justice&#160;– not those of cult prophets or professional ecstatics, but of morally upright prophetic leaders in the tradition of Moses, Joshua, Deborah, Gideon, and others ..." 141</span></li>

<li id="cite_note-ndq-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ndq_45-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ndq_45-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">"[...] die griechische Bibelübersetzung, die einem innerjüdischen Bedürfnis entsprang [...] [von den] Rabbinen zuerst gerühmt (.) <li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The translation, which shows at times a peculiar ignorance of Hebrew usage, was evidently made from a codex which differed widely in places from the text crystallized by the Masorah." <cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://jewishencyclopedia.com/articles/3269-bible-translations">"Bible Translations – The Septuagint"</a>. JewishEncyclopedia.com<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">10 February</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABible&amp;rft.btitle=Bible+Translations+%E2%80%93+The+Septuagint&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=JewishEncyclopedia.com&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fjewishencyclopedia.com%2Farticles%2F3269-bible-translations&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Two things, however, rendered the Septuagint unwelcome in the long run to the Jews. Its divergence from the accepted text (afterward called the Masoretic) was too evident; and it therefore could not serve as a basis for theological discussion or for homiletic interpretation. This distrust was accentuated by the fact that it had been adopted as Sacred Scripture by the new faith [Christianity] [...] In course of time it came to be the canonical Greek Bible [...] It became part of the Bible of the Christian Church."<cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://jewishencyclopedia.com/articles/3269-bible-translations">"Bible Translations – The Septuagint"</a>. JewishEncyclopedia.com<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">10 February</span> 2012</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABible&amp;rft.btitle=Bible+Translations+%E2%80%93+The+Septuagint&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.pub=JewishEncyclopedia.com&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fjewishencyclopedia.com%2Farticles%2F3269-bible-translations&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mishnah Sotah (7:2–4 and 8:1), among many others, discusses the sacredness of Hebrew, as opposed to Aramaic or Greek. This is comparable to the authority claimed for the original Arabic Koran according to Islamic teaching. As a result of this teaching, translations of the Torah into Koine Greek by early Jewish <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">Rabbis</a> have survived as rare fragments only.</span></li>

<p><span class="reference-text">But if anyone receive not, as sacred and canonical, the said books entire with all their parts, as they have been used to be read in the Catholic Church, and as they are contained in the old Latin vulgate edition; and knowingly and deliberately <a href="/wiki/Contemn" class="mw-redirect" title="Contemn">contemn</a> the traditions aforesaid; let him be <a href="/wiki/Anathema" title="Anathema">anathema</a>.</span></p>

<li>Taylor, Hawley O. "Mathematics and Prophecy." <i>Modern Science and Christian Faith</i>. Wheaton: Van Kampen, 1948, pp.&#160;175–83.</li>
<li><i>Wycliffe Bible Encyclopedia</i>, <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sv#English" class="extiw" title="wikt:sv">s.vv.</a> "Book of Ezekiel", p.&#160;580 and "prophecy", p.&#160;1410. Chicago: Moody Bible Press, 1986.</li>
</ul>
